<figcaption>\n  Illustration via \n  <a href=\"http://99designs.com/tim\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">99designs</a>\n </figcaption>\n</figure> \n<blockquote class=\"wp-block-quote\">\n <p><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong>“I actually don’t that often think about the details of how bad the scientific projections are and exactly what’s happening to ecosystems. I focus almost entirely on solutions.”</strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></p>\n <cite>—&nbsp;Dr. Ayana Elizabeth Johnson</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p><a href=\"https://www.ayanaelizabeth.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><strong>Dr. Ayana Elizabeth Johnson</strong></a> (<a href=\"https://twitter.com/ayanaeliza\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">@ayanaeliza</a>) is a marine biologist, policy expert, writer, and Brooklyn native. She is co-founder of <a href=\"http://bit.ly/UrbanOceanLab\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Urban Ocean Lab</a>, a think tank for coastal cities, and co-creator of the Spotify/Gimlet podcast <a href=\"https://gimletmedia.com/shows/howtosaveaplanet\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>How to Save a Planet</em></a>, on climate solutions. She co-edited the bestselling climate anthology <a href=\"http://www.allwecansave.earth/anthology\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>All We Can Save</em></a> and co-founded <a href=\"http://allwecansave.earth/project\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">The All We Can Save Project</a>.</p> \n<p>Recently, she co-authored the <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://collectiv.in/BND-WarrenPlan\" target=\"_blank\">Blue New Deal</a>, a roadmap for including the ocean in climate policy. Previously, she was executive director of the Waitt Institute, developed policy at the EPA and NOAA, and taught as an adjunct professor at New York University. Dr. Johnson earned a BA in environmental science and public policy from Harvard University and a PhD in marine biology from Scripps Institution of Oceanography.</p> \n<p>She publishes widely, including in <a href=\"http://bit.ly/NYT-UrbanOcean\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>The New York Times</em></a>, <a href=\"https://bit.ly/WaPoClimateBLM\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>The Washington Post</em></a>, and <a href=\"http://bit.ly/SciAm-AEJ\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Scientific American</em></a>. She is on the 2021 <a href=\"https://time.com/collection/time100-next-2021/5937679/ayana-elizabeth-johnson/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">TIME100 Next List</a> and was named one of <em>Elle</em>‘s <a href=\"https://collectiv.in/Elle27\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">27 Women Leading the Charge to Protect Our Environment</a>. <em>Outside</em> magazine <a href=\"https://bit.ly/OutsideMag2\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">called her</a> “the climate leader we need.”</p> \n<p>Please enjoy!</p> \n<p><strong><em><strong><em>Listen to the doing much public-facing stuff right now, and why did she choose to make an exception for this conversation? [06:59]</li>\n <li>When did Ayana know marine biology and conservation were in her blood, and that she could actually pursue a career that wove all of her dream jobs together? [10:33]</li>\n <li>What superpowers did Ayana inherit from her parents? [17:18]</li>\n <li>Why do snorkeling and scuba diving provide such a transformative experience for so many humans? How does this drive home the immediacy of our climate crisis in a way that can’t be ignored? [26:31]</li>\n <li>Why are Ayana’s parents the coolest? [32:34]</li>\n <li>How can we get people excited about course-correcting climate change from a perspective of awe and wonder instead of hopeless guilt? [35:42]</li>\n <li>Can Ayana guess which episode of her podcast I recently shared with my 5-Bullet Friday readers and why? [42:01]</li>\n <li>Can Ayana guess which episodes of her podcast I might be hesitant to share? [48:53]</li>\n <li>On the need for linguistic jiu-jitsu mastery siding with climate solutions, and why Ayana’s been keen on using the word “transformation.” [51:06]</li>\n <li>How can we empower ourselves to make a difference against a never-ending number of environmental and societal catastrophes? What’s the right mindset for trying to solve problems that seem way bigger than us? How can we stay energized to fight the good fight without succumbing to apathy — or even just general disappointment in our fellow humans? [54:44]</li>\n <li>Thoughts on the promising technologies and companies vying to provide solutions to our environmental problems. [1:02:31]</li>\n <li>No matter who you are or what you do, there’s room for you to be part of the solution. [1:08:04]</li>\n <li>Could humor be the secret weapon against climate crisis? [1:11:06]</li>\n <li>How can we get both sides of the political spectrum to cooperate on legislation needed to fight environmental crises? [1:13:24]</li>\n <li>As a constituent, what’s the outsized cheat code you can use for ensuring your voice matters to your elected congresspeople? [1:19:16]</li>\n <li>What are the next actions we can take to goad politicians into taking more aggressive steps in stemming environmental crises? [1:25:23]</li>\n <li>What can someone who has a general aversion to politics do to get involved in changing policy? [1:29:27]</li>\n <li>How choosing not to eat shrimp is just one example of a small decision that makes a huge difference. [1:32:48]</li>\n <li>How Ayana came to be cool with her name. [1:40:23]</li>\n <li>Audience asks and parting thoughts. [1:41:14]</li>\n</ul> \n<h3 id=\"more-guest-quotes-from-the-interview\">MORE GUEST QUOTES FROM THE INTERVIEW</h3> \n<p><strong>“It doesn’t need to be a partisan issue. We can all care about maintaining a habitable planet.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Ayana Elizabeth Johnson</br></p> \n<p><strong>“People don’t think about the full spectrum of ways you can be a part of the solution. You can be a tycoon and do this. You can be a tech nerd and do this. You can be a technologist and do this. You can be a health nut and do this. Whatever is your thing, we need you.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Ayana Elizabeth Johnson</br></p> \n<p><strong>“There’s a lot of money to be made in green energy, in food system shifts, in huge transportation. All of it.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Ayana Elizabeth Johnson</br></p> \n<p><strong>“We need more climate media that is deploying humor, because humor, quantitatively we know, is a way to have people open their minds to new ideas. When you’re laughing, you’re not fighting. You’re able to listen. It helps you let your guard down. It helps you be in community with people in a different way.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Ayana Elizabeth Johnson</br></p> \n<p><strong>“The majority of Americans understand that climate change is a massive problem. It’s only nine percent of Americans who are full-blown climate science deniers.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Ayana Elizabeth Johnson</br></p> \n<p><strong>“I actually don’t that often think about the details of how bad the scientific projections are and exactly what’s happening to ecosystems. I focus almost entirely on solutions.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Dr. Ayana Elizabeth Johnson</br></p> \n<h3 id=\"people-mentioned\">PEOPLE MENTIONED</h3> \n<ul>\n <li><a <p><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong>“If you’re going to live a deep life, the ultimate original commitment is, ‘I’m going to commit to discipline in the sense of things I am going to do on a regular basis, because they matter, even if I don’t feel like it.’ And that is the biggest binary zero-to-one flip that happens in crafting a life.”</strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></p>\n <cite>—&nbsp;Cal Newport</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p><strong>Cal Newport</strong> (<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.calnewport.com/\" target=\"_blank\">calnewport.com</a>) is an associate professor of computer science at Georgetown University who previously earned his PhD from MIT. His scholarship focuses on the theory of distributed systems, while his general-audience writing explores intersections of culture and technology.</p> \n<p>Cal is the author of seven books, including, most recently, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Deep-Work-Focused-Success-Distracted/dp/1455586692\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Deep Work</em></a>, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Digital-Minimalism-Choosing-Focused-Noisy/dp/0525536515\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Digital Minimalism</em></a>, and <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.amazon.com/World-Without-Email-Reimagining-Communication/dp/0525536558\" target=\"_blank\"><em>A World Without Email</em></a>. He is also a contributing writer for <em>The New Yorker</em> and the host of the <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.calnewport.com/podcast/\" target=\"_blank\"><em><strong>Deep Questions</strong></em></a> podcast.</p> \n<p>Please enjoy!</p> \n<p><strong><em><strong><em>Listen to the episode on <a Today, everyone understands it, but until a minute ago, people thought I was literally insane.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Cal Newport</br></p> \n<p><strong>“We tell ourselves that we’re stuck in this way of existence. We’re not really stuck. We have a lot of options.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Cal Newport</br></p> \n<p><strong>“If you’re going to live a deep life, the ultimate original commitment is, ‘I’m going to commit to discipline in the sense of things I am going to do on a regular basis, because they matter, even if I don’t feel like it.’ And that is the biggest binary zero-to-one flip that happens in crafting a life.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Cal Newport</br></p> \n<p><strong>“For me, a day off is a day where I have full autonomy over what I do. The blank calendar day, to me, is one of the more glorious sights.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Cal Newport</br></p> \n<p><strong>“We’re using these things all the time because it’s more palatable to be active and busy all the time than sometimes to face what’s hard about what’s going on in our lives or the world.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Cal Newport</br></p> \n<p><strong>“Don’t be afraid of ideas or worldviews or approaches or philosophies that seem different than what you believe in. You’re not going to get tricked out of your convictions or believe in something that’s false. You actually strengthen your understanding of the world and therefore strengthen your own convictions by encountering other ideas that are well-formed.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Cal Newport</br></p> \n<p><strong>“We’re good, as humans, to committing to things that are positive. That’s very motivating for us. We’re bad at trying to avoid things that are negative.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Cal Newport</br></p> \n<p><strong>“Schedule shut down, complete.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Cal Newport</br></p> \n<h3 id=\"people-mentioned\">PEOPLE MENTIONED</h3> \n<ul>\n <li><a href=\"https://twitter.com/SteveMartinToGo\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Steve Martin</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://mobile.twitter.com/charlierose\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Charlie Rose</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.arthurconandoyle.com/sherlockholmes.html\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Sherlock Holmes</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://tim.blog/2019/09/03/david-allen-getting-things-done/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">David Allen</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephen_Covey\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Stephen Covey</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"http://www.annpatchett.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Ann Patchett</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.nobelprize.org/prizes/physics/1965/feynman/biographical/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Richard P. <figcaption>\n  Illustration via \n  <a href=\"http://99designs.com/tim\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">99designs</a>\n </figcaption>\n</figure> \n<blockquote class=\"wp-block-quote\">\n <p><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong>“The reason I resist closed boxes is that nature does not deal in closed boxes.”</strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></p>\n <cite>—&nbsp;Margaret Atwood</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p><strong>Margaret Atwood</strong> (<a href=\"https://twitter.com/margaretatwood\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">@margaretatwood</a>) is the author of more than 50 books of fiction, poetry, critical essays, and graphic novels. <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Dearly-New-Poems-Margaret-Atwood-ebook/dp/B0847NZL76\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Dearly</em></a>, her first collection of poetry in over a decade, was published November 2020. Her latest novel, <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Testaments-Novel-Margaret-Atwood-ebook/dp/B07KVLPYDQ\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>The Testaments</em></a>, is a co-winner of the 2019 Booker Prize. It is the long-awaited sequel to <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Handmaids-Tale-Margaret-Atwood/dp/1328879941\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>The Handmaid’s Tale</em></a>, now an award-winning TV series. Her other works of fiction include <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Cats-Eye-Margaret-Atwood-ebook/dp/B00513F9Q6\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Cat’s Eye</em></a>, finalist for the 1989 Booker Prize; <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Alias-Grace-Novel-Margaret-Atwood-ebook/dp/B00513F9PM\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Alias Grace</em></a>, which won the Giller Prize in Canada and the Premio Mondello in Italy; <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Blind-Assassin-Novel-Margaret-Atwood/dp/0385720955\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>The Blind Assassin</em></a>, winner of the 2000 Booker Prize; the <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/MADDADDAM-TRILOGY-BOX-Crake-Maddaddam/dp/0804172315\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">MaddAddam Trilogy</a>; and <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Hag-Seed-William-Shakespeares-Tempest-Shakespeare-ebook/dp/B01AQO0IHY\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Hag-Seed: William Shakespeare’s </em>The Tempest<em> Retold</em></a>.</p> \n<p>Margaret’s work has been published in more than 45 countries, and she is the recipient of numerous awards, including the Peace Prize of the German Book Trade, the Franz Kafka International Literary Prize, the PEN Center USA Lifetime Achievement Award, and the Los Angeles Times Book Prize Innovator’s Award.</p> \n<p><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Burning-Questions-Essays-Occasional-Pieces-ebook/dp/B09635CJ9V\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><strong><em>Burning Questions</em></strong></a>, a collection of essays from 2004–2021 will be published in March of this year. <a href=\"https://studio.disco.co/learn-live-with-margaret-atwood-course\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><strong>Practical Utopias: An Exploration of the Possible</strong></a>, an eight-week, live, online learning experience, will run later this year.</p> \n<p>Please enjoy!</p> \n<p><strong><em><strong><em>Listen to the episode on <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" [24:24]</li>\n <li>What drives Margaret’s ability to craft engaging speculative fiction? [26:51]</li>\n <li>What are the downsides of raising a family in the woods, blissfully isolated from the world? Margaret shares a glimpse into her own childhood. [38:03]</li>\n <li>How the limited number of career options from which a young woman was expected to choose guided Margaret toward her current profession — and how long it took to start paying off. [44:17]</li>\n <li>What benefit did Margaret get from writing during the time before being paid to do so? [49:44]</li>\n <li>As someone who’s often found herself in the teaching profession, what type of teaching has Margaret enjoyed most? [52:59]</li>\n <li>Why Margaret considers&nbsp;<em>The Future of Life</em>&nbsp;by Edward O. Wilson to be required reading for young adults. [55:28]</li>\n <li>Why Margaret resists the act of labeling that humans tend toward. [58:24]</li>\n <li>What explains Margaret’s ongoing interest in dystopian — as well as utopian — literature, and what can people expect from “Practical Utopias: An Exploration of the Possible,” her eight-week online learning experience? [1:02:58]</li>\n <li>Comparing and contrasting major revolutions and political upheavals of recent centuries, and what Margaret learned by visiting Eastern Bloc countries during the Cold War. [1:08:31]</li>\n <li>How is the DISCO online learning platform that will host “Practical Utopias: An Exploration of the Possible” different from other such platforms, and what kind of problems will participants be solving? [1:12:01]</li>\n <li>What readers can expect from&nbsp;<em>Burning Questions</em>. [1:14:42]</li>\n <li>How has Margaret’s writing process changed over the course of her life? What does it look like these days? [1:19:24]</li>\n <li>A tangent about shows we binge when our writing quotas for the day are fulfilled, an H.G. Wells story about perspective, and a&nbsp;<em>Twilight Zone</em>&nbsp;episode that (surprise!) doesn’t end well for its protagonist. [1:22:04]</li>\n <li>Tezos NFTs, illustrated utopias, and inventions fitting unexpected functions. [1:24:22]</li>\n <li>A spoiler alert for anyone who hasn’t yet read&nbsp;<em>The Testaments</em>&nbsp;and doesn’t want to know what happens to a character from&nbsp;<em>The Handmaid’s Tale</em>: skip ahead to the next timestamp! [1:31:48]</li>\n <li>Does Margaret do research for her characters? [1:33:27]</li>\n <li>Margaret turns the tables and asks me what prompted my podcasting endeavors. [1:35:36]</li>\n <li>Dictation apps, the three Henry Jameses, and confessional stenographers. [1:37:48]</li>\n <li>Undertaking winter adventures at high elevations and other parting thoughts. [1:41:25]</li>\n</ul> \n<h3 id=\"more-guest-quotes-from-the-interview\">MORE GUEST QUOTES FROM THE INTERVIEW</h3> \n<p><strong>“You bring to any book who you already are and the age that you are and the experience that you’ve had, and it’s the same for everyone.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Margaret Atwood</br></p> \n<p><strong>“The reason I resist closed boxes is that nature does not deal in closed boxes.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Margaret Atwood</br></p> \n<p><strong>“There I was in my pink princess line dress crossing the football field, and a poem occurred to me. It wasn’t a very good poem, but it was a poem. I was very excited about it. And this is how these things start. You write some pretty terrible poetry that you’re very excited about, and luckily there’s nobody there to tell you, ‘This is really terrible poetry,’ and then you go on from there.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Margaret Atwood</br></p> \n<p><strong>“I was going to be a botanist because I was actually quite good at it. But then along came this writing, much to my parents’ dismay. But being the bite-your-tongue kind of parents, I think they just hoped it would be a phase that I would grow out of.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Margaret Atwood</br></p> \n<p><strong>“Writers make stuff up. You ask them questions that essentially have no answers, but they make stuff up anyway. I’ll tell you what I made up, but it is kind of true.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Margaret Atwood</br></p> \n<h3 id=\"people-mentioned\">PEOPLE MENTIONED</h3> \n<ul>\n <li><a href=\"https://poets.org/poet/homer\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Homer</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.worldhistory.org/odysseus/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Odysseus</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.thecollector.com/penelope-odyssey-heroine/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Penelope</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.thecanadianencyclopedia.ca/en/article/grace-marks\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Grace Marks</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Jung\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Carl Jung</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://mobile.twitter.com/realsarahpolley\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Sarah Polley</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.theglobeandmail.com/life/margaret-dorothy-killam-atwood/article684022/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Margaret Dorothy Killam Atwood</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://eeb.utoronto.ca/support-us/carl-atwood-graduate-award/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Carl Edmund Atwood</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_de_Mestral\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">George de Mestral</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Queen_Victoria\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Queen Victoria</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.poetryfoundation.org/poets/t-s-eliot\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">T.S. changed my behavior over the last year. I revisit them often.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>They were all featured in <strong><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://go.tim.blog/5-bullet-friday-1/?utm_source=timblog&#38;utm_medium=timblog&#38;utm_campaign=quotes-im-pondering\" target=\"_blank\"><em>5-Bullet Friday</em></a></strong>, my free weekly newsletter, which is a short email of five bullet points sent out each Friday to ~1.5–2M subscribers. Each edition describes the five coolest things I’ve found or explored that week, often including books, gadgets, tricks from experts, articles, and weird stuff from all over the world.&#160;&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>I hope you enjoy the following gems as much as I have&#8230;</p>\n\n\n\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\"/>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“Language can become a screen which stands between the thinker and reality. This is the reason why true creativity often starts where language ends.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthur_Koestler\">Arthur Koestler</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“I imagine one of the reasons people cling to their hates so stubbornly is because they sense, once hate is gone, they will be forced to deal with pain.”</strong><br>― <a href=\"https://el2.convertkit-mail2.com/c/zluwknl73vunhoedw3/kncmh6h5gkd4nx/aHR0cHM6Ly9lbi53aWtpcGVkaWEub3JnL3dpa2kvSmFtZXNfQmFsZHdpbg==\">James Baldwin</a>, <a href=\"https://el2.convertkit-mail2.com/c/zluwknl73vunhoedw3/2es2hoh8rkvn3q/aHR0cHM6Ly9lbi53aWtpcGVkaWEub3JnL3dpa2kvTm90ZXNfb2ZfYV9OYXRpdmVfU29u\"><em>Notes of a Native Son</em></a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“People fall so in love with their pain, they can’t leave it behind. The same as the stories they tell. We trap ourselves.”</strong><br>—<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chuck_Palahniuk\"> Chuck Palahniuk</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“The truth knocks on the door and you say, ‘Go away, I&#8217;m looking for the truth,’ and so it goes away. Puzzling.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Robert_M._Pirsig\">Robert M. Pirsig</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong><strong>“</strong>Character, like a photograph, develops in darkness.<strong>”</strong></strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yousuf_Karsh\">Yousuf Karsh</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“A man is about as big as the things that make him angry.”</strong><br><strong>―</strong> <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winston_Churchill\">Winston Churchill</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“I will have to remember ‘I am here today to cross the swamp, not to fight all the alligators.’”</strong><br>— From <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Art-Possibility-Transforming-Professional-Personal/dp/0142001104\"><em>The Art of Possibility</em></a> by <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Rosamund-Stone-Zander/e/B001KIUW8Q\">Rosamund</a> and <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Benjamin_Zander\">Benjamin</a> Zander</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“Our bodies are apt to be our autobiographies.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gelett_Burgess\">Frank Gelett Burgess</a></p>\n\n\n\n<span id=\"more-50833\"></span>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“Anytime you&#8217;re practicing renunciation, you&#8217;re deluded. How about <em>that!</em> You&#8217;re deluded. What are you renouncing? Anytime you renounce something, you are tied forever to the thing you renounce. There&#8217;s a guru in India who says, ‘Every time a prostitute comes to me, she&#8217;s talking about nothing but God. She says I&#8217;m sick of this life that I&#8217;m living. I want God. But every time a priest comes to me, he&#8217;s talking about nothing but sex.’ Very well, when you renounce something, you&#8217;re stuck to it forever. When you fight something, you&#8217;re tied to it forever. As long as you&#8217;re fighting it, you are giving it power. You give it as much power as you are using to fight it.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthony_de_Mello\">Anthony de Mello</a>, <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Awareness-Opportunities-Reality-Anthony-Mello/dp/0385249373\"><em>Awareness</em></a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“If we can forgive what&#8217;s been done to us&#8230; If we can forgive what we&#8217;ve done to others&#8230; If we can leave our stories behind. Our being victims and villains. Only then can we maybe rescue the world.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chuck_Palahniuk\">Chuck Palahniuk</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“All of our miseries are nothing but attachment.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rajneesh\">Osho</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“Without courage we cannot practice any other virtue with consistency. We can&#8217;t be kind, true, merciful, generous, or honest.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maya_Angelou\">Maya Angelou</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“Those who do not weep, do not see.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victor_Hugo\">Victor Hugo</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“What do you despise? By this are you truly known.”</strong><br>―<a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_Herbert\"> Frank Herbert</a>, <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dune_(novel)\"><em>Dune</em></a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“To the economically illiterate, if some company makes a million dollars in profit, this means that their products cost a million dollars more than they would have cost without profits. It never occurs to such people that these products might cost several million dollars more to produce if they were produced by enterprises operating without the incentives to be efficient created by the prospect of profits.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Sowell\">Thomas Sowell</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“A serious and good philosophical work could be written consisting entirely of jokes.”</strong><br>― <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ludwig_Wittgenstein\">Ludwig Wittgenstein</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“You remain awake at night and perform your devotions. Also awake at night are dogs, better than you. They bark and in no way can they be stopped. They go and sleep on the dung heap, better than you. They do not leave their master&#8217;s door, even if they get beaten with slippers, better than you. Bulleh Shah, buy yourself something for the journey, or else the game will be won by the dogs, better than you.”</strong><br>― <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulleh_Shah\">Bulleh Shah</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“A wealth of information creates a poverty of attention.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon\">Herbert Simon</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong><strong>“</strong>I regard consciousness as fundamental. I regard matter as derivative from consciousness. We cannot get behind consciousness. Everything that we talk about, everything that we regard as existing, postulates consciousness.<strong>”</strong></strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Max_Planck\">Max Planck</a> </p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“Of all sexual aberrations, chastity is the strangest.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatole_France\">Anatole France</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“Almost everything will work again if you unplug it for a few minutes, including you.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anne_Lamott\">Anne Lamott</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“I believe no man was ever scolded out of his sins.”</strong><br><strong>— </strong><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Cowper\">William Cowper</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“The secret to doing good research is always to be a little underemployed. You waste years by not being able to waste hours.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amos_Tversky\">Amos Tversky</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“No person would give up even an inch of their estate, and the slightest dispute with a neighbor can mean hell to pay; yet we easily let others encroach on our lives—worse, we often pave the way for those who will take it over. No person hands out their money to passersby, but to how many do each of us hand out our lives! We’re tight-fisted with property and money, yet think too little of wasting time, the one thing about which we should all be the toughest misers.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger\">Seneca the Younger</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong><strong>“</strong>Be who you are and say what you feel, because those who mind don&#8217;t matter and those who matter don&#8217;t mind.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dr._Seuss\">Dr. Seuss (Theodor Seuss Geisel)</a> (alternatively attributed to Sir Mark Young and/or Bernard Baruch)</p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“Do not spoil what you have by desiring what you have not; remember that what you now have was once among the things you only hoped for.”</strong><br><strong>― </strong><a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Epicurus\">Epicurus</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“Old George Orwell got it backward. Big Brother isn&#8217;t watching. He&#8217;s singing and dancing. He&#8217;s pulling rabbits out of a hat. Big Brother’s busy holding your attention every moment you&#8217;re awake. He&#8217;s making sure you&#8217;re always distracted. He&#8217;s making sure you&#8217;re fully absorbed. He&#8217;s making sure your imagination withers. Until it&#8217;s as useful as your appendix. He&#8217;s making sure your attention is always filled. And this being fed, it&#8217;s worse than being watched. With the world always filling you, no one has to worry about what&#8217;s in your mind. With everyone&#8217;s imagination atrophied, no one will ever be a threat to the world.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chuck_Palahniuk\">Chuck Palahniuk</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“The first principle is that you must not fool yourself—and you are the easiest person to fool.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Feynman\">Richard Feynman</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“It is by logic that we prove, but by intuition that we discover.”</strong><br>― <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9\">Henri Poincaré</a></p>\n\n\n\n<p><strong>“To doubt everything or to believe everything are two equally convenient solutions; both dispense with the necessity of reflection.”</strong><br>— <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9\">Henri Poincaré</a></p>\n\n\n\n<hr class=\"wp-block-separator\"/>\n\n\n\n<p><em><strong><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://go.tim.blog/5-bullet-friday-1/?utm_source=timblog&#38;utm_medium=timblog&#38;utm_campaign=quotes-im-pondering\" target=\"_blank\">You can subscribe to </a></strong></em><strong><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://go.tim.blog/5-bullet-friday-1/?utm_source=timblog&#38;utm_medium=timblog&#38;utm_campaign=quotes-im-pondering\" target=\"_blank\">5-Bullet Friday</a></strong><em><strong><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://go.tim.blog/5-bullet-friday-1/?utm_source=timblog&#38;utm_medium=timblog&#38;utm_campaign=quotes-im-pondering\" target=\"_blank\"> here</a>. Illustration via \n  <a href=\"http://99designs.com/tim\">99designs</a>\n </figcaption>\n</figure> \n<blockquote class=\"wp-block-quote\">\n <p><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong><strong>“If it had been a horror movie, people in the audience would’ve started saying, ‘Don’t go near the shadowy place!’ And of course, as I walked past the shadowy place, I actually sat down just on the edge of those shadows. And my perception was that the water was too shallow for crocodiles. But of course the crocodile was in the hole, and the first thing that you notice when a crocodile grabs you is just the ferocity and the pressure of the bite.”</strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></strong></p>\n <cite>—&nbsp;Boyd Varty</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p><strong>Boyd Varty</strong> (<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://twitter.com/boydvarty\" target=\"_blank\">@boydvarty</a>) is the author of two books, <strong><a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Lion-Trackers-Guide-Life/dp/0358099773\" target=\"_blank\"><em>The Lion Tracker’s Guide to Life</em></a> </strong>and his memoir, <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Cathedral-Wild-African-Journey-Home/dp/1400069858\" target=\"_blank\"><em>Cathedral of the Wild</em></a>. He has been featured in <em>The New York Times</em>, on NBC, and in other media and has taught his philosophy of “tracking your life” to individuals and companies around the world.</p> \n<p>Boyd is a wildlife and literacy activist who has spent the last ten years refining the art of using wilderness as a place for deep introspection and personal transformation. He grew up in South Africa on <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.londolozi.com/\" target=\"_blank\">Londolozi Game Reserve</a>, a former hunting ground that was transformed into a nature preserve by Boyd’s father and uncle—both visionaries of the restoration movement. Under his family’s stewardship, the Reserve became renowned not only as a sanctuary for animals but as a place where once-ravaged land was able to flourish again and where the human spirit could be restored. When Nelson Mandela was released after 27 years of imprisonment, he went to Londolozi to recover.</p> \n<p>Boyd has a degree in psychology from the University of South Africa. He is a <a href=\"https://www.ted.com/talks/boyd_varty_what_i_learned_from_nelson_mandela\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">TED speaker</a> and the host of the <a href=\"https://podcasts.apple.com/us/podcast/track-your-life-with-boyd-varty/id1505181324\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Track Your Life</em> podcast</a>.</p> \n<p>Please enjoy!</p> \n<p><strong><em><strong><em>Listen to the episode on <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" [05:44]</li>\n <li>How the Londolozi Game Reserve came to be, and what happened during Boyd’s childhood that instilled him with a “get on with it” attitude. [08:02]</li>\n <li>Why did Boyd’s father and uncle insist on keeping the property that would become the Londolozi Game Reserve when, at the time, it was considered useless, overgrazed wasteland? [10:18]</li>\n <li>Boyd shares what it was like growing up as a regular passenger/survivor of The White Knuckle Charter Company. [12:25]</li>\n <li>How a man named Ken Tinley and the native Shangaan trackers helped a trio of teenagers transform their expanse of scrub-encroached land into a thriving safari business. [21:03]</li>\n <li>On the ancient lineage of the Shangaan trackers, and how the local wildlife came to trust the human caretakers of Londolozi. [27:05]</li>\n <li>Renias Mhlongo is supreme among world-class trackers — and sometimes the importance of the work outweighs the will of his clients. [32:21]</li>\n <li>Which animals are hardest to track at Londolozi — even if you happen to be Richard Siwela? [37:53]</li>\n <li>Because nature can be unpredictable, how do people protect themselves in Londolozi? [41:03]</li>\n <li>“I don’t know where we’re going, but I know exactly how to get there.” —Renias Mhlongo [42:56]</li>\n <li>How the tracking process has changed for Boyd over the years — from confident child to young adult traumatized by a home invasion and crocodile attack to competent grown-up thanks to people like Dr. Martha Beck and Solly Mhlongo. [45:00]</li>\n <li>What is Ubuntu? [1:02:50]</li>\n <li>Boyd talks about that time he lived 40 days and 40 nights up a tree — the questions he was trying to answer for himself by doing so, the primal fear he experienced while waiting out a storm, the pros and cons of extreme solitude, and if he’d do it again. [1:05:15]</li>\n <li>Stories about bees, the birds who help humans rob them, and the power of the hive algorithm. [1:17:45]</li>\n <li>The dos and don’ts of interacting with lions in the wild. [1:29:42]</li>\n <li>On the eerie conversation of death, modern confirmation of ancient myths, and the inexplicable movements of beasts and men. [1:34:16]</li>\n <li>How did Boyd’s own path toward healing after trauma differ from the way his mother and sister recovered from a trauma they experienced? [1:39:57]</li>\n <li>What is ceremony work, and how can it help someone deal with trauma? [1:43:32]</li>\n <li>What Boyd means when he says “an authentic life infused with meaning is a kind of activism.” [1:46:40]</li>\n <li>How Boyd and I have both been affected by the Work of Byron Katie. [1:52:03]</li>\n <li>Boyd’s first medicine encounter in an Arizona sweat lodge, and what he took away from the experience. [1:56:31]</li>\n <li>Feelings. Nothing more than feelings. [2:02:49]</li>\n <li>Kudus and nightjars and leopards in the fire (oh, my)! What a close encounter with a beautiful predator taught Boyd about Ubuntu. [2:04:14]</li>\n <li>Examining the therapeutic value of spending time with animals. [2:13:05]</li>\n <li>Laurens van der Post poetically described the sound of a lion’s roar. And, in a packed presentation hall at a major Silicon Valley company, Boyd did not. [2:17:57]</li>\n <li>An invitation to visit Londolozi and other parting thoughts. [2:22:10]</li>\n</ul> \n<h3 id=\"more-guest-quotes-from-the-interview\">MORE GUEST QUOTES FROM THE INTERVIEW</h3> \n<p><strong>“Biologists feel the more time you spend in nature, the more you’ll realize how little we know. There is subtlety and nuance and there are things happening out there that go way beyond our understanding.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Boyd Varty</br></p> \n<p><strong>“In these times, an authentic life infused with meaning is a kind of activism.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Boyd Varty</br></p> \n<p><strong>“You can’t half-roar at a group of executives. You’ve either got to not do it or go all in.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Boyd Varty</br></p> \n<p><strong>“It was a little Cessna that had a quirk. And let me tell you, when it comes to aviation, you don’t want planes with quirks.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Boyd Varty</br></p> \n<p><strong>“If it had been a horror movie, people in the audience would’ve started saying, ‘Don’t go near the shadowy place!’ And of course, as I walked past the shadowy place, I actually sat down just on the edge of those shadows. And my perception was that the water was too shallow for crocodiles. But of course the crocodile was in the hole, and the first thing that you notice when a crocodile grabs you is just the ferocity and the pressure of the bite.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Boyd Varty</br></p> \n<p><strong>“If you spend time in nature in the same spot, over a period of time, it starts to become incredibly personal. So it’s not just a bird or that antelope, it’s that bird that roosts in that bush and flies down the river bed in the morning and back up the southern bank. And then it feeds for grubs in this tree. And as you start to become more personally attuned to each animal, you start to see that there’s a pattern to their movement. And in fact, then you start to find yourself orientated inside of a series of interlocking intelligences—that is really what the natural world is.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Boyd Varty</br></p> \n<h3 https://i0.wp.com/tim.blog/wp-content/uploads/2022/02/OSIRIS_Mars_true_color.jpeg?w=1800&#38;ssl=1 1800w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 767px) 90vw, 600px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" /><figcaption>True color image of Mars taken by the OSIRIS instrument on the ESA Rosetta spacecraft during its February 2007 flyby of the planet. </figcaption></figure>\n\n\n\n<p>Before we live on Mars, will we need to prove that we can live underground?<br><br>This thought experiment and possible path is found in futurist <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://amywebb.io/\" target=\"_blank\">Amy Webb</a>&#8216;s (<a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://twitter.com/amywebb\" target=\"_blank\">@amywebb</a>) new book <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Genesis-Machine-Rewrite-Synthetic-Biology/dp/1541797914\" target=\"_blank\"><strong><em>The Genesis Machine: Our Quest to Rewrite Life in the Age of Synthetic Biology</em></strong></a>, coauthored with microbiologist and geneticist <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Andrew_Hessel\" target=\"_blank\">Andrew Hessel</a>. Renowned computer scientist <a href=\"https://tim.blog/2020/04/21/rana-el-kaliouby/\">Rana el Kaliouby</a> describes the book as a “roadmap for this interdisciplinary field of synthetic biology that is forever reshaping life as we know it.”&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Part Three of the book explores different futures in the form of fictional, speculative scenarios.<br><br>These scenarios describe how the world may develop, given what we know to be true today. These are near-term plausible developments, reasonable extensions of current trend lines, not sci-fi. They are also opportunities to rehearse the future, and these structured thought exercises are used by everyone from military strategists to CEOs.&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Scenario #4, “The Underground,” is excerpted from <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Genesis-Machine-Rewrite-Synthetic-Biology/dp/1541797914\"><em>The Genesis Machine</em></a> with permission and with light edits for length.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Please enjoy!&#160;</p>\n\n\n\n<h2 class=\"has-text-align-center\" id=\"the-underground\">The Underground</h2>\n\n\n\n<p>For decades, Elon Musk, CEO of Tesla and SpaceX, had been insisting that humanity’s best shot at long-term survival was to eventually become a multiplanetary species. He cited elevated levels of carbon pooling in the Earth’s atmosphere, extreme droughts, and the loss of biodiversity as precursors to a looming catastrophe. He began development on a program called Starship in 2016, which was intended to ferry cargo and, eventually, one hundred passengers between the Earth, Moon, and Mars. By 2021, NASA contracted SpaceX to develop a modified Starship vehicle for its Artemis program. Musk focused on building the core infrastructure that would eventually be required to sustain life, whether on Earth or on the Moon, Mars, or even beyond. But Musk realized he could not build an off-planet living environment on his own. Ever the showman, and with his personal fortune approaching $1 trillion, Musk announced an audacious contest called the Colony Prize. He’d award $1 billion to any team that could build and operate an underground, airtight colony of one hundred people for two years. In other words, the ultimate Mars simulation.<sup>1,2,3</sup></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Musk knew that for humans to thrive off-planet, regenerative systems would need to be developed at a scale never before achieved.<sup>4</sup> The International Space Station once housed as many as thirteen astronauts on board, but typically only six or seven lived in the ISS at once. Colonists also needed to grapple with long periods of confinement. The typical ISS mission was about six months.<sup>5</sup> NASA astronaut Scott Kelly had spent nearly a year in space. Cosmonaut Valeri Polyakov held the record for a single mission, spending an impressive 437 days on the Mir station in the 1990s.<sup>6</sup> A better example for understanding a hundred-person enclosed society was a submarine––but even here the longest submerged and unsupported mission topped out at 111 days.<sup>7</sup> Winning the Colony Prize would require keeping the doors sealed tight for more than 700 days.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The contest rules were simple by design. Entrants were to outfit and assemble airtight canisters into a closed living environment. Those canisters, which would begin as empty, modular self-contained spaces that would fit into the cargo bay of a rocket, could be configured as living quarters, science labs, farms, schools, water treatment systems, manufacturing facilities, and anything considered necessary to support a community. Colonies were encouraged to include facilities to support concerts, sports, and other forms of recreation.<sup>8</sup> Once configured and loaded with supplies, the doors would be sealed and the mission clock started. The goal wasn’t to reinvent Buckminster Fuller’s geodesic dome. It was to invent entirely new networks of modular structures—using something akin to the Minneapolis Skyway System, the world’s largest contiguous system of enclosed structures and bridges—that could scale to eventually become a city. Over time, such a plan would replicate some aspects of life in the days before extreme weather became our new normal.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>In addition to container reconfiguration plans and simulations, contest entrants were told to submit a list of potential colony inhabitants, justification for their selection, and a detailed plan to ensure quality of life. There was an important caveat: the colony couldn’t just be made up of a bunch of carefree early twenty-somethings who, back in the halcyon days of the aughts, might have attended Coachella. Every colony had to mirror the full spectrum of society: a mix of families, couples without children and single people. The prize was intended, in part, to test population expansion in a closed system. That meant that facilities to manage pregnancy, delivery, and infant care, as well as various health issues and the various stages of life, had to be built.<sup>9</sup></p>\n\n\n\n<span id=\"more-59755\"></span>\n\n\n\n<p>There were no requirements or quotas to ensure diversity of thought, ideology, race, ethnicity, nationality, or culture. Nor were there stipulations to prevent certain people from being excluded from a colony. If a group could prove in simulation that its plan could support life for two years, and if they could explain how inhabitants would work, attend school, receive medical attention, cultivate resources, and maintain balance within the colony, they’d be eligible to advance.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Selected teams would have eleven years to build, refine, and live within their structures. In the event of a system failure or need to make major changes to a configuration, any colony was allowed to reset the clock and start again, provided they had enough time remaining within the eleven-year limit.<sup>10</sup> There was no limit to how many colonies could win the $1 billion award on successful completion.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Colonies would receive support from Musk’s various companies—SpaceX, Tesla, The Boring Company (his tunnel and underground infrastructure company), Chia (the energy-efficient blockchain and smart transaction platform), NovoFarm (an indoor, precision agriculture company), Neuralink (the implantable brain-machine interface company), and Programmable Matter (maker of materials that can shape-shift to respond to the environment or user input).<sup>11,12,13</sup> Feasibility studies and infrastructure pathfinders had been completed, so a crucial aspect was location: future Mars colonies would need to be built underground. Mars lacks a magnetic field and radiation levels on the surface are dangerously high. The surface is cold. Building underground would provide radiation shielding and thermal insulation.<sup>14,15,16</sup></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Tunnels would be made by The Boring Company. Its automated Prufrock V machines could “porpoise,” meaning they could be launched from the surface, tunnel underground at a rate of almost one mile a day, and then surface after completion. Tesla produced stainless steel cylinders that fit neatly into these tunnels. They were like shipping containers, except that they were shaped like canisters of Pringles chips and had electric drive systems so they could move slowly under their own power. The interior of the canisters could be customized to house virtually anything, such as private quarters, hydroponic farms, or surgical facilities. They could operate independently for a short period, but they would typically be linked together to form more complex systems, the most straightforward configuration being a chain, like a subway train. Tesla also built solar and battery systems, while SpaceX handled transportation and communications with its Starlink satellites. The companies had installed systems on the moon under its contract with NASA. Colonies would have plenty of electricity and bandwidth.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Colony Prize teams were allowed to use this research to support their designs. Digital plans, models, and specifications were available online, and empty canisters were available for purchase from Tesla for $250,000 each. The big challenge for teams would be putting together, populating, and operating complete systems.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Musk made it clear, in his instructions to those vying for the Colony Prize, that ambition would be rewarded:</p>\n\n\n\n<p><em>The goal is to create habitable places not just to live but live well. Build the colony where you and your family can thrive, with the right kind of people, and consider how it might continue to grow to become fully self-sustaining.</em></p>\n\n\n\n<p>Colonists were required to self-fund their plans, including paying the salaries of those developing the colony and salaries for the colonists themselves. The $1 billion prize for successful colonies would be used to reimburse investors, pay out bonuses, and possibly fund further expansion. Musk believed that this model would incentivize and promote cooperation among various colonies, which would create a flywheel for innovation and accelerate a space-based economy, plus provide real-world experience on governance and operations.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The sheer magnitude of the prize—along with the weather on the surface [of Earth], which had become extremely unpleasant—catalyzed an enormous global investment in closed living system R&#38;D. The only example that came close was by then fifty years old: the Biosphere 2 in Oracle, Arizona, which completed construction in 1991.<sup>17</sup> Originally intended to demonstrate the viability of closed ecological systems, Biosphere 2 was ultimately plagued with problems. Too little food, poor oxygen circulation, and a power struggle over the project’s management and administration doomed the experiment. No one since had tried to integrate the tremendous progress that had happened since then in vertical farming, manufacturing, sensor systems, and biotech into another closed system.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>While there were tens of thousands of applications, only 180 proposals passed the initial rounds of filtering. They came from North America, Western Europe, United Korea, China, and India in what became known as colony-forming units, or CFUs. Getting started required that CFUs produce detailed plans and models for water regeneration, biofoundries, medical treatment, oxygen generation, and carbon capture. Doing so required real ingenuity and extensive computer-aided modeling. Ultimately, seventy-two CFUs had built skilled teams and secured land for their colony and surface operations. All of them had secured enough funding—ranging from government grants to investment from private companies to checks written by wealthy donors—to begin building.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Tesla began shipping thousands of canisters and power systems to colonies, which included such varied locales as Bloomington, Indiana, and Humboldt, Iowa; Dalmeny, Saskatchewan, and Edmonton, Alberta (Canada); Hwaseong (United Korea); Beizhen and Dadongzhen (China); Harda (India); Rumuruti (Kenya); and Knutsho (Norway). Along with partners, teams began customizing canisters and linking them together to form what, from a distance, could be mistaken for high-tech hamster enclosures, setting them up on the surface first and testing them extensively in preparation for moving underground.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>While the prize rules did not limit the number of awards, it did set strict performance milestones. To meet milestones, colonies had to engineer microbes, including bacteria, that enabled crops to fertilize themselves. Sustainable indoor farms, which included climate-controlled environments, cloud-based AI systems, agricultural sensors, and collaborative robotics, were required to prove they could maintain safe levels of nutrition, carbon dioxide, oxygen, and hydration. Teams also had to design, build, test, and deploy DIY vaccines and therapies to manage any novel pathogen that might arise in the enclosed environment. Auxiliary products intended for everyday use—such as intelligent packaging made of polymers that effectively self-destruct or “unzip” when exposed to light, heat, or acid—were necessary to meet stringent waste management criteria.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>At first, the teams struggled to meet their milestones. Creating a sustainable canister for one family to survive for a few years was hard enough. Scaling it to an entire community, and having some semblance of normal life deep underground was a far more complicated endeavor. Colony teams quickly realized the best strategy was cooperation, since there was no cap to the number of winners. Once they started sharing what they’d learned, the engineering of key colony systems evolved incredibly quickly. It didn’t take long for the teams to arrive at configurations that computer simulations predicted would support 100 people, then 150, then almost 200. They all also realized that it was important to build in some redundancy. Things go wrong, such as equipment failures. And sometimes things go right. Colony populations were expected to grow during the mission experiment.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>By January 2043, just six years into the experiment, the first colony, Endeavor Sub Terra, announced that it was ready to seal the doors and start the mission clock. Endeavor Sub Terra’s community (ESTers, as they became known) was situated just east of the Arizona State University campus, beyond the Maricopa First Nations Community. (Ironically enough, Biosphere 2 had stood nearby the Arizona State campus, too.) It was sponsored in part by the university and the state government, which provided land and generous tax incentives. ESTers were carefully selected from the large community that had formed to build the Arizona colony. Many were families with kids, though there were young couples and people in various other relationship configurations. They’d all been living and working in the canisters for some time already. Going on the clock just meant not going outside for more than 730 days.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Its canisters were moved underground; the tunnel was sealed and filled with gases that closely matched the Martian atmosphere composition. Power and communication systems simulated the expected kilowatts and also transmission delays, which ranged from three minutes to as long as twenty-two minutes, depending on the relative positions of the planets.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>ESTers were the first colonists to seal themselves off from the surface, but because of the extensive information and infrastructure sharing, most of the others were close behind. By the spring of 2044, all seventy-two colonist teams had moved underground.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>CFUs devised and used different economic and governance systems. Some paid colonists as full-time employees, who earned salaries for the time they spent working on the prize and living in the community. Like the International Space Station, there was nothing to buy or sell. Salaries earned were deposited into colonists’ bank accounts for their use back on the surface. Other colonies developed universal basic income (UBI) models, where all inhabitants received a set of credits to start with in the form of community digital tokens. Gradually, community members would use these tokens as currency— to pay for goods and services while they lived in the colony.<sup>18</sup></p>\n\n\n\n<p>There were detractors. Some people referred to the colonies as “ant farms,” “hamster cages,” and “self-filling prisons.” But the colonists shrugged off the barbs. They believed their canisters and tunnels were great places to live, work, and raise a family. The environment was free from pathogens. Extreme surface weather events weren’t even noticed underground. Tunnels proved safe during the firenados that were ravaging large swaths of North America and Western Europe in the summer of 2044.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The colonies excelled at bioengineering. Their life science canisters were equipped with the best biofabs, including sequencers and synthesizers. Those in charge of developing organisms needed for vertical farms and recycling systems invented novel approaches, and they adapted and evolved their local, natural ecosystems as time passed. They also designed special surveillance systems to detect any contaminations or mutations.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The underground colonies provided refuge from perilous surface storms, but the experiment didn’t change basic human nature. Before sealing, psychographic data were collected on all community members to ensure they could withstand living in an enclosure with just 99 other people, but no one accurately predicted the ideal community composition. Neurodiverse candidates were allowed, though people with panic disorder or attention-deficit/hyperactivity disorder (ADHD), or who were prone to depression, were strongly discouraged. Those who had anger management issues, or who displayed signs of narcissistic personality disorder, were typically excluded. Still, some community leaders bent the rules, or outright broke them. Wealthy donors expected access and privileges in return for their investment, which often meant jumping the line in front of more qualified or suitable candidates. Some donors even bought their teenagers a colony stint, hoping it would land their kids at more prestigious colleges later. Others thought it would be the ultimate status vacation, or a way to boost traffic to their virtual media channels, and insisted they make the list.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>There were also failures. In some colonies, vicious politicking, infighting, and scandal plagued inhabitants the minute the doors closed. In Visionary Valley, for example, the funders were determined to manage the community as they would a business. Within two months, the colony imploded. The funders had insisted that only they should know the lock codes to key resources, such as food and water stores. They also built a colony-wide surveillance system that could be viewed using only their own biometric authentication. This wasn’t known to colony members in advance, who realized, once they were all underground, that a hierarchical system was in place that mirrored the power and wealth imbalance they’d endured on the surface.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The colonists attempted a coup, but they were effectively living in a panopticon, and there was no way for them to take over. Disgusted and enraged, they broke the seal on Visionary Valley and vowed never to return.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>In every community, some colonists struggled with social isolation, the abrupt change of lifestyle, and restricted movement. Several felt a sense of persistent unease, which led to difficulty concentrating and sleeping. For others, depression and anxiety were more acute. Those colonists became easily frightened and developed paranoia. Some had violent outbursts or became detached from family members and friends. Colonists gave that condition a name—traumatic below- surface syndrome, or TBSS—and there was no easy way to treat it.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>The most successful colonies were those that acknowledged humanity’s basic physiological and safety needs. People wanted to feel a sense of purpose and belonging, and there were plenty of jobs to do within each community. A few UBI programs were successful, but most digital token systems weren’t perfect. Colonists blew through their initial allotment fast, and there was no bank to lend them additional credits. They had to borrow from neighbors, which caused friction, as it always has. In one colony, a sudden surge in demand for strawberries led to inflation, temporarily causing the prices of all produce to spike.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Flat power structures rarely work; some people will always want to lead, and others never do. Many colonies developed a modified social democratic system of government favoring consensus. Colony administrators rotated through positions, which wasn’t always perfect, but incentivized administrators against leaving a mess for their successors. Several colonies experimented with letting AI systems run everything.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Endeavor Sub Terra, the first to go on the clock, was also the first to win the $1 billion award in early 2045. Musk and the Colony Prize would eventually award fifty-five of the seventy-two teams. He considered it to be the best return on investment he’d ever made. Humanity had built the technical and social foundation for becoming a multiplanetary, spacefaring species—one that could scale indefinitely when given access to energy and raw materials. In addition to producing net surpluses of food, water, and other necessities, many colonies had reached economic escape velocity: the research, systems, and products they created were earning them a lot of money on the surface. If they wanted to, they could reinvest and keep growing. Which is why many ESTers decided to remain underground even after the mission ended.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>They had developed an airlock and decontamination system that would enable colonists to pop back up to the surface on occasion to visit old friends or to enjoy one of the few days of good weather. They agreed to wearing or ingesting sensors, colony-wide testing, and quarantines to ensure that no one brought a virus or other pathogen back into public areas in the enclosure. They purchased their own tunneling machines and additional canisters to accommodate another two thousand people—but they already had a third growth plan underway for millions of colonists, with new underground neighborhoods, geothermal generators, massive bioreactors, and even an underground ocean. It may not have been his intention, but Musk’s Colony Prize had seeded the largest investment in sustainable communities that humanity had ever seen.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Around the world, surface ecosystems were rewilding as struggling farms and towns were abandoned for the underground. Buildings, roads, and homes were left to degrade naturally, eroded by sunlight, water, and vegetation. Nature and natural systems were bouncing back faster than anyone predicted, which required a new generation of naturalists and ecologists to study Earth’s dramatic new ecosystem shifts. For the first time in over a century, the CO2 levels in the atmosphere began to drop.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>ESTers could see a future of flexible living: a way for people to live well on spaceship Earth or, if desired, off-planet. A personal module could be shipped to Mars and connected to a colony.</p>\n\n\n\n<p>Sometimes ESTers would visit the surface at night. Lying on the ground, and without any light pollution, they would marvel at the canopy of stars above them. The stars seemed to whisper as they twinkled: <em>Come, humans, explore!<br><br></em>Mars, and other planets, were waiting.<br><br><strong>Excerpted from </strong><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Genesis-Machine-Rewrite-Synthetic-Biology/dp/1541797914\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><strong><em>THE GENESIS MACHINE: Our Quest to Rewrite Life in the Age of Synthetic Biology</em></strong></a><strong><em> </em>by Amy Webb and Andrew Hessel. Copyright © 2022. Available from PublicAffairs, an imprint of Hachette Book Group, Inc.</strong></p>\n\n\n\n<p><em>Footnotes: </em></p>\n\n\n\n<p>1. Complacent</strong>.”</strong></strong></p>\n <cite>— Bill Rasmussen</cite>\n</blockquote> \n<p>Welcome to another episode of <a href=\"http://tim.blog/podcast\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>The Tim Ferriss Show</em></a>, where it is my job to deconstruct world-class performers to tease out the routines, habits, et cetera that you can apply to your own life.</p> \n<p>You’ll get plenty of that in this special episode, which features <a href=\"https://youtu.be/WsbtHlce95U\">my interview with Bill Rasmussen</a> from my 2017 TV Show <em>Fear{less}</em>. The “less” is in parentheses because the objective is to teach you to fear <em>less</em>, not to be fearless.</p> \n<p><em>Fear{less}</em> features in-depth, long-form conversations with top performers, focusing on how they’ve overcome fears and made hard decisions, embracing discomfort and thinking big.</p> \n<p>It was produced by Wild West Productions, and I worked with them to make both the video and audio available to you for free, my dear listeners. You can find <a href=\"https://youtu.be/WsbtHlce95U\">the video of this episode</a> on <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.youtube.com/timferriss\" target=\"_blank\">YouTube.com/TimFerriss</a>, and eventually you’ll be able to see all episodes for free at <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" href=\"https://www.youtube.com/timferriss\" target=\"_blank\">YouTube.com/TimFerriss</a>.</p> \n<p>Spearheaded by actor/producer and past podcast guest <a href=\"https://tim.blog/2017/05/31/vince-vaughn/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Vince Vaughn</a>, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/wildwest\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Wild West Productions</a> has produced a string of hit movies including <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Internship-Vince-Vaughn/dp/B00F55HNBY\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>The Internship</em></a>, <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Couples-Retreat-Vince-Vaughn/dp/B009CFYVJK\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Couples Retreat</em></a>, <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Four-Christmases-Vince-Vaughn/dp/B0091X5IWM\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Four Christmases</em></a>, and <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Break-Up-Vince-Vaughn/dp/B009CFY4NS\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>The Break-Up</em></a>.</p> \n<p>In 2020, Wild West produced the comedy <a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Opening-Act-Jimmy-Yang/dp/B08K39LBD2\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>The Opening Act</em></a>, starring Jimmy O. Yang and Cedric The Entertainer. In addition to <em>Fear{less}</em>, their television credits include <a href=\"https://audiencenetwork.att.com/audience/undeniable/season-5\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>Undeniable with Joe Buck</em></a>, ESPN’s <a href=\"https://plus.espn.com/30-for-30\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>30 for 30</em> episode</a> about the ’85 Bears, and the Netflix animated show <a href=\"https://www.netflix.com/title/80028732\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\"><em>F is for Family</em></a>.</p> \n<p>Please enjoy!</p> Ury, and Bruce Patton | Amazon</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Secrets-Power-Negotiating-25th-Anniversary/dp/1632651963\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Secrets of Power Negotiating, 25th Anniversary Edition by Roger Dawson | Amazon</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.esquire.com/entertainment/interviews/a9097/jodie-foster-interview-0111/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Jodie Foster: What I’ve Learned | Esquire</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/December-1941-Changed-America-Saved/dp/159555582X\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">December 1941: 31 Days that Changed America and Saved the World by Craig Shirley | Amazon</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"http://hometownnetworks.tv/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">Live Stream Sports and Entertainment | Hometown Networks</a></li>\n <li><a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/4-Hour-Workweek-Escape-Live-Anywhere/dp/0307465357\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\">The 4-Hour Workweek: Escape 9-5, Live Anywhere, and Join the New Rich by Tim Ferriss | Amazon</a></li>\n</ul> \n<h3 id=\"show-notes\">SHOW NOTES</h3> \n<ul>\n <li>A recap of ESPN’s opening night. [05:34]</li>\n <li>What accounts for Bill’s lifelong obsession with sports? [07:41]</li>\n <li>How Bill’s family — especially his father — encouraged him to excel from an early age. [09:46]</li>\n <li>By the time he got to college, what did Bill imagine he would do for a career — and what ultimately put a crimp in his ambitions? [11:20]</li>\n <li>What did Bill learn during his initial foray into the working world, and how did this lead to the start of his first business? [13:47]</li>\n <li>How did Bill segue from this first business into the world of sports broadcasting? Was his lack of experience at the beginning any hindrance? [17:01]</li>\n <li>What does Bill’s self-talk sound like when he’s trying to convince himself to try something new? [21:33]</li>\n <li>As confident as Bill is in trying new things, he’s not lived a life without self-doubt-inducing setbacks. Here’s one that led to the much bigger success that would become ESPN. [23:04]</li>\n <li>What ESPN looked like in the conceptual stages — when its financial reserve consisted of a $9,000 credit card advance. [29:40]</li>\n <li>How Bill and his team drummed up additional funds for the fledgling network. [31:50]</li>\n <li>The clever reason behind shrink-wrapping the early NCAA pitch deck. [35:29]</li>\n <li>Enter Walter Byers. [37:07]</li>\n <li>What launch day and the days leading up to it looked like. [39:06]</li>\n <li>The best decision made at ESPN. [41:32]</li>\n <li>24 hours, seven days a week is a lot of television to fill. What are some of the more esoteric ways that time has been filled at ESPN? [42:33]</li>\n <li>Why did Bill leave ESPN at 2:05 p.m. on June 25th, 1984? [44:08]</li>\n <li>Bill’s secret to effective pitching and negotiation, and a few book recommendations. [45:09]</li>\n <li>ESPN has the power to bring people together — except for when someone needs a scapegoat for divorce proceedings. [46:44]</li>\n <li>What does success mean to Bill, and who does he think of when he hears the word? Does he consider himself a success? [47:45]</li>\n <li>If Bill had to give a high-profile, 20-minute talk about something unrelated to what made him famous, what would the topic be? What book would he recommend to someone who wanted to learn more about this topic? [48:57]</li>\n <li>Current challenges. [50:19]</li>\n <li>Bill’s best advice for someone seeking to launch their own business. [52:00]</li>\n <li>What Bill’s billboard would say. [53:17]</li>\n</ul> \n<h3 id=\"more-bill-rasmussen-quotes-from-the-interview\">MORE BILL RASMUSSEN QUOTES FROM THE INTERVIEW</h3> \n<p><strong>“ABC, NBC: Always Be Curious, Never Be Complacent.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Bill Rasmussen</br></p> \n<p><strong>“Keep your eye on the ball. Keep focused. Never, never let up until you’re accomplished what you want to do.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Bill Rasmussen</br></p> \n<p><strong>“I was getting old. I was approaching 30. I didn’t want my life to go by without something. Well, when I found out I couldn’t play baseball, I decided that I could become a broadcaster.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Bill Rasmussen</br></p> \n<p><strong>“Years ago, tales of Jules Verne and Buck Rogers were made of dreams and wild bits of imagination. Today, modern technology has taken those dreams and that imagination and turned it into a reality that allows us to bring a television picture into your home via satellite.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Bill Rasmussen</br></p> \n<p><strong>“He said, ‘You’re kidding me. You don’t have a tape. You’ve never done it, but you know you can do it?’ I said, ‘Yep.’ He said, ‘You know what? Based on this approach, I’m putting a news station on the air in Amherst, Massachusetts. And if you’ll help me put it on the air, you’re my sportscaster.'”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Bill Rasmussen</br></p> \n<p><strong>“If you’re a fan, what you’ll see in the next minute, hours, and days to follow may convince you you’ve gone to sports Heaven. Beyond that blue horizon is a limitless world of sports, and right now you’re standing on the edge of tomorrow, sports 24 hours a day, seven days a week from ESPN, the total sports cable network.”</strong><br>—&nbsp;Bill Rasmussen</br></p> \n<p><strong><em><strong><em>Listen to the episode on <a rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" Mullenweg&nbsp;</strong>(<a href=\"https://twitter.com/photomatt\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" target=\"_blank\">@photomatt</a>) is a co-founder of the open-source publishing platform&nbsp;<a href=\"https://wordpress.com/\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" target=\"_blank\">WordPress</a>, which now powers more than one-third of all sites on the web. He is the founder and CEO of&nbsp;<a href=\"https://automattic.com/\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Automattic</a>, the company behind WordPress.com,&nbsp;<a href=\"https://woocommerce.com/\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" target=\"_blank\">WooCommerce</a>,&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.tumblr.com/\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Tumblr</a>,&nbsp;<a href=\"https://wpvip.com/\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" target=\"_blank\">WPVIP</a>,&nbsp;<a href=\"https://dayoneapp.com/\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Day One</a>, and&nbsp;<a href=\"https://www.pocketcasts.com/\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Pocket Casts</a>. Additionally, Matt runs&nbsp;<a href=\"https://audrey.co/\" rel=\"noreferrer noopener\" target=\"_blank\">Audrey Capital</a>, an investment and research company. He has been recognized for his leadership by&nbsp;<em>Forbes, Bloomberg Businessweek, Inc.</em>,<em>&nbsp;TechCrunch</em>,<em>&nbsp;Fortune</em>,<em>&nbsp;Fast Company</em>,<em>&nbsp;Wired</em>,&nbsp;<em>Vanity Fair</em>, and the University Philosophical Society.&nbsp;</p> \n<p>Matt is originally from Houston, Texas, where he attended the High School for the Performing and Visual Arts and studied jazz saxophone. In his spare time, Matt is an avid photographer. He currently splits his time between Houston and Jackson Hole.</p> \n<p>For my first interview with Matt, way back in 2015, go to&nbsp;<a href=\"https://tim.blog/2015/02/09/matt-mullenweg/\"
